The Center Year Book
1963-64
5724
Jacksonville
1903 - 6o T'ears
The history of the Jacksonville Jewish Center dates
back over sixty years to the founding of the Congregation
B’nai Israel in 1901. At the time of its incorporation in
1903 this congregation had grown from five to forty
families. When in 1907 the membership had increased
to seventy-five, the congregation felt strong enough to
begin the “erection of a suitable edifice to house its
religious and educational activities.55
At the end of the first quarter of the century, it be¬
came apparent that a Jewish Center was necessary to
reverse the “deplorable indifference to the . . . education
of the young. The continuance of this55, said Rabbi
Samuel Benjamin, “would have caused the absolute ex¬
tinction of the local Jewish community.55 To prevent this
he proposed the founding of the Jacksonville Jewish
Center which would serve not only one congregation, but
the entire Jewish community in all its religious, social,
cultural and physical needs.
And so it was that in 1927 the Congregation B’nai
Israel became the Congregation of the Jacksonville
Jewish Center under the leadership of Rabbi Benjamin
and Mr. Harry Finkelstein, its first President. The new
congregation, relocated at its present site at Third and
Silver Streets, became a Conservative Synagogue affiliated
with the United Synagogue of America. Since that time
Jewish Center
m
of Progress - 1963
it has played a “major role in the existence and develop¬
ment of practically every Jewish group in the city.55
The year 1947-48 brought further evidence of the
need for expansion in order to meet the needs of our
youth in the community. In 1950 Groundbreaking cere¬
monies were held for the Activities Building, and the
dedication was celebrated in April of the following year.
The dedication of the Youth Center marked the begin¬
ning of the finest youth program in this part of the
country. At the same time plans were made for the
complete renovation of the sanctuary. This part of the
expansion program was completed and dedication was
observed in May of 1958.
When the need for several more classrooms became
most pressing, the Building Fund Committee began to
plan still further expansion so that every pupil could be
provided with a proper classroom. This project was
realized in 1959 when the addition to the school building
was completed)
The Center now stands with its building program
completed, but with an eye toward the future: In 1962
the Land Acquisition Committee was formed so that the
Congregation’s growing needs may best be served. Thus,
the Center has grown from a congregation of five families
to one of over eight hundred and has become one of the
greatest symbols of progressive Judaism in the Southeast.

In Memory Of
Hnbi'rí 3L (inriimt
With sincere gratitude we humbly dedicate this Year Book to the late
Robert J. Gordon,, of blessed memory.
RESOLUTION
Adopted November 8, 1962, at Board Meeting
WHEREAS, The Jacksonville Jewish Center stands bowed in grief along with the
family of our late beloved and revered synagogue leader,
Robert J. Gordon, Alav ha-Shalom
WHEREAS: The Jacksonville Jewish Center feels its own inability to record in
detail and to adequately evaluate the great accomplishments, fine contribution
and abiding influence of Robert J. Gordon, and
WHEREAS: The congregation feels spiritually compelled to express its sorrow for
this irreplaceable loss and whereas Robert J. Gordon served for nearly two
decades in almost every responsibility in our congregation, serving as Treasurer,
Chairman of the Board, Chairman of the Building Fund and serving closely with
the standing committee who needed his services, and
WHEREAS: It was his personality that served as an inspiration to all who worked
with him, being a source of harmony and good will when differences endangered
our unity, and
WHEREAS: It was the bigness of his outlook that motivated him to support even
those projects that he did not favor, for in his own eyes his own desires were not
as important as the general welfare of the congregation, and
WHEREAS: In the light of his tremendous achievements and in the light of his
profound influence, his own genuine humility motivated him to shun all honor and
refuse all credit for great accomplishment, and
WHEREAS: It was his unique status in the eyes of his fellow members and his own
generous contribution of time, effort and finances that permitted him to influence
others to make ever greater sacrifices on behalf of the congregation, and
WHEREAS: The efforts of Robert J. Gordon are reflected in the progress and
success of almost every Jewish, fraternal, philanthropic and cultural organization
in this community, be it therefore
RESOLVED: We, the Officers, Board of Directors and Trustees of the Jacksonville
Jewish Center in meeting assembled, deplore his passing and extend our deepest
heartfelt sympathy to his wife, Lillian Gordon, his sons, Samuel and Alan, his
grandchildren, and other members of his family.
BE IT FURTHER RESOLVED: That this Resolution be spread on the minutes of this
meeting and a copy be forwarded to members of the family.

We of the Administration staff would like to take this opportunity of extending to Rabbi Arnold Turetsky and
his family a sincere welcome to our community and congregation. In the short time since the Rabbi has assumed the
position his warm personality and leadership have given us greater incentive in the administration work of the Center.
We take this means of extending our best wishes for a Happy and Prosperous Year to the congregation and its lay
leadership. “LE SHONAH TOVAH TIKATEVU”
As one of the outstanding Synagogue-Centers in the Southeast, we offer our community a dynamic program cover¬
ing every phase of religious, educational, social, cultural and athletic programming. Throughout this book a brief sum¬
mary in pictures and words depicts our year-round activities with a brief birds-eye view of our overall program found
on page 43. We invite your comments concerning our operation and know that your continued support will provide us
with the necessary impetus to continue this vital role in our community.
Our loyal maintenance staff consists of Tim Riley and
Wallace Mazon with Annie Mae McNeil in charge of
the kitchen.
Our bjis drivers are Eugene Davis, Willie Baldwin and
Morris Montford. They are shown in front of one of the
four busses operated by the Center to provide transporta¬
tion for our School, Day Camp, Services and many other
activities.

A Message From Our Rabbi
Israel
July 17, 1963
The bus from Eilat to Mitzpeh Rimon is not air
conditioned. I am writing and riding in a dry
Negev heat of abouf|||150. Hot winds and sand
come through the open windows. Some of the more
resourceful (and less thirsty) passengers have
- saturated towéls with canteen water to cover their
windows, fashioning primitive: air conditioning
units. The road is narrow and bumpy. It is the
second day of the Negev Tiyul and we are tired,
dusty, dry, and very rumpled. Our feet carry
souvenirs of a painful attempt to swim with sea
urchins in the coral bedded Gulf of Aqaba.
It is too uncomfortable to sleep so I am report¬
ing on my fellow passengers. Their reactions to
disagreeable conditions are interesting. The boy
sitting behind me is studying “dikduk”. Back of
him a young fellow is reading a book on Jewish
mysticism. From the last seat comes an endless
stream of accordion and harmonica music which
picks up groups of singers, one after another. The
song never ends. I have not heard a grumble or a
complaint.
One would expect nothing less from trial tested
Israelis.
But the grammer student is from Philadelphia,
the mysticist likewise, and the accordionist from
Houston. They are your children U.S.Y.
Pilgrimage 1963, 116 teenagers from the United
States and Canada. Were they at home, the girls
from Detroit would be getting up for breakfast at
this hour, before going to the lake. They were
davening Shaharit this morning at 5:30.
RABBI ARNOLD S. TURETSKY
Their visit in Israel will influence their lives
permanently. You couldn’t be a serious Jew and
fail to be impressed. But they are influencing Israel
too. They stand critical inspection before Israelis at:
every turn, and the image of American Jewry
which they present should make you very grateful.
I am grateful to them for something else, too.
They insure the future strength of our kind of
Judaism in America. They are not merely members
of Conservative Synagogues, they are Conservative
Jevys. There’s a difference.
Because of them, tomorrow will be better than
today —- higher ideals, stronger faith. With every
year we come closer.
May this year bring to us, our loved ones, and
all the House of Israel life and health, peace and
quiet, and the incredible joy of Jewish fulfillment.
Rabbi Arnold S. Turetsky

From Strength to Strength
Rabbi Moshe V. Goldblum, our Rabbi for the past two
years, has recently left our Synagogue for the pulpit of Con¬
gregation Beth Shalom in Pittsburgh, which is the largest Con¬
servative Congregation in that city. While the congregation
was sorry to hear of this decision, we were happy for Rabbi
Goldblum’s personal advancement to the large metropolitan
synagogue.
His two years of service at the Jacksonville Jewish Center
were marked with much devotion and dedication to the needs
of all our congregants. His warm personality and friendly smile
quickly won the admiration and friendship of our members. The
Rabbi and his personable wife Evelyn will long be remembered
for the devoted way in which they instilled Judaism among our
members. We know that Congregation Beth Shalom will also
receive great inspiration from their association with Rabbi
Goldblum.
Almost completely through the tireless efforts of Rabbi
Goldblum and his wife, the Foundation School was re-organized
and begun after a lapse of several years. The Rabbi’s continued
devotion also played a significant part in our weekly Shalos
Seudos services and study sessions.
We wish them much Mazal and success in their new
endeavors. RABBI MOSHE V. GOLDBLUM

Message From The Center President
When I wrote my first message as President some 12
years ago, I could not have envisioned the tremendous de¬
velopments which are now taking place nor could I have
imagined the horizons which have now been opened to us.
One therefore wonders whether even our present visions of
the future are not too limited, realizing that all things are
possible to those who desire them.
Certainly there are limitations to what can be achieved
but the greatest limitation is our own actions in setting of
goals which are inadequate. Rarely does one achieve more
than his goal. An inadequate goal pre-determines the cer¬
tainty of failure.
Had our congregation been content with its educational
system of ten years ago, there would today be no Founda¬
tion School, no Hebrew High School, no Ramah program,
no well attended junior congregation system, no Sunday
morning religious school system continuing through the
high school level, etc. Had we been content with our build¬
ing facilities we would have been out of existence by now
for lack of facilities. Had we been content with our general
activities, there would be no extensive youth program or
successful Day Camp facility such as we now have. Had we
been content with our religious program, there would be no
daily Minyan, no year-around late Sabbath evening services,
no improvement in holiday services observances, etc.
Can we, then, from these lessons of the past, dare to
become content with what we now have? One cannot stand
still. He either goes forward or as he stands still, time
passes him by. Even more so is this true of the synagogue.
The future achievements within our grasp could not pos¬
sibly have been envisioned a decade ago or five years ago,
or perhaps even a year ago.
With the purchase of our new 36 acre tract on State
Road 13, there opens before us new vistas never before even
dreamed of. Not only can we plan for the utilization of this
tract for permanent building facilities to ultimately replace
our present ones, but even while planning for these facilities
so many other possibilities present themselves.
We are already committed to the installation of our Day
Camp on this site. This will mean an expanded Day Camp
Outgoing President Louis Safer and Burton I. Gross-
man, Chairman of the Board, at the Installation,
PHILIP SELBER, President
program. It can make possible a division of the camp which
can be operated partially on the level of a Camp Ramah.
It can afford excellent additional outdoor facilities for our
youth programs, whether it be Girl Scouts, Boy Scouts, Cub
Scouts, U. S. Y. activities or others. It can mean a place
where adult education retreats can be held over the Sabbath
weekend. It can mean possible experimentation with out¬
door religious services during the summer. It can mean
weekend recreational activities for our members and for our
affiliated social clubs.
Perhaps not as dramatic but just as important are the
many educational opportunities awaiting a synagogue which
dares to go forward. With the success of our Foundation
School, the standards of the intensive department of our
school systems can now be tripled and quadrupled. Coupling
this with an intensive High School Department and a sum¬
mer Camp Ramah program, the future attainments of our
Hebrew School students will be far beyond anything which
we can now contemplate.
With the new emphasis being placed on adult education,
future achievements in this area will be limited only by our
own goals. It is possible to re-educate our adults to the
point that 75% of our members would be able to follow in¬
telligently Hebrew services and to have a sound basic ap¬
preciation of Conservative Judaism. With this type of re¬
training program, will come changes in religious observances
which we would not even dare contemplate today.
Certainly there are problems in the way of these achieve¬
ments, but there are none which cannot be overcome by
our desire to achieve.
On behalf of the Officers and members of the Board of
Directors and the Board of Trustees, may I extend a greet¬
ing to all of our members for a Happy New Year, in the
traditional words of “Leshanah Tovah Tikatevu”.

Cantor And Choir
Center Choral Society
The 30 voice adult Choral Society composed of Center members which performs at various Center and community
functions throughout the year.
The Center Choral Society and Glee Club, pictured on this page along
with our music Director Abraham Marton, conducted the 15th Annual Con¬
cert staged at the Jacksonville Jewish Center. This annual event is held in
conjunction with National Jewish Music Month and is dedicated to the
Cantors’ Institute of the Jewish Theological Seminary of America.
This past year’s Concert held on March 3rd featured “Avodath Hako-
kesh”, the Sacred Service by Bloch, and was attended by over 500 from the
community. The entire Center membership looks forward to this annual event
at which time our Choral Society and Glee Club are highlighted along with
outstanding guest soloists. This year’s soloists included Edward Doe, Karen
Armel, Paul Vasvari, Roselyn Klein and Sam Sherter and members of the
St. Johns Opera Co., with Jonathan Bowers as pianist.
CANTOR A. MARTON
Center Glee Club
Boys and girls from our Hebrew School and Sunday School make up this 70 voice Glee Club which meets throughout
the year and performs at many community celebrations and Center festivities.

Center Sisterhood
Each Sisterhood member is the object of our interest.
Our total program is directed to help educate the Jewish
woman who will then stimulate the Jewish education of her
family, thus enriching her home, the Synagogue and the
Jewish community.
Our purpose in the words of Mathilda Schechter, who
founded the National Women’s League of the United
Synagogue of America, is: “T o serve the cause of
Judaism by strengthening the bond of unity among Jewish
women, to appreciate everything fine in Jewish life and
literature and to instill the beauty of our ancient observances
in the hearts of our children ... to cherish the various
ceremonies of Sabbath and the holidays ... to teach their
significance intelligently. . . .”
We of the Center Sisterhood try to continue in the pro¬
gram as set forth by our founder. The year 1962-63 was
started by bringing to our membership the urgent need for
building the Mathilda Schechter Residence Hall in New
York City, to house girls who attend the Seminary to be¬
come our future Hebrew teachers. During September we
also had a very successful one-day work shop which was
presented by Mrs. William Dickson, President of the Florida
Branch of National Women’s League and her committee.
An exciting first was the Art Exhibit chaired by Mrs.
William H. Schemer and Mrs. Harry Yoffee featuring
original paintings and crafts by Sisterhood and Center mem¬
bers. Over 70 pieces of art were displayed and added a great
deal of interest to our Donor Affair.
“Breakfast With The Rebitzin”, )a brunch held at the
home of Rabbi and Mrs. Moshe V. Goldblum to honor our
Torah Fund contributors was a social as well as financial
success. We easily completed our Torah Fund quota which
was sent to the Jewish Theological Seminary. This fund
helps to maintain the Seminary and its many projects such
as scholarships for Rabbis and Cantors, the Eternal Light
program, the Jewish Museum, Library, etc.
Not to be overlooked was the very interesting Social
Action program presenting the book “Some of My Best
Friends” by Benjamin R. Epstein and Arnold Forster. A
very lively discussion followed the review by Mr. Joel Gold¬
man and Mrs. Al L. Schneider. Also of interest was a
“Shabbos on Wednesday” presented at our April meeting.
Sisterhood works very closely with the two United Syna¬
gogue Youth groups of the Center and sponsors the Girl
Scouts and Brownie troops.
The annual Florida Branch Conference was held in
Scenes from our first annual Art Exhibit.
An Oneg Shabbat was held after services October 6 and
“The Slave” by Isaac Beshevis Singer was reviewed by
Mrs. Isadore Moscovitz. Also on the program were represen¬
tative students who attended Camp Ramah and the Leader¬
ship Training Institute of the United Synagogue Youth.
The annual Membership Tea brought us a delightful
fashion show featuring Convention fashions as this was the
year of the Women’s League National Convention held at
the Concord Hotel at Lake Kiamisha, N.Y. and attended by
Mrs. Louis Slott.
December featured the annual congregational Chanukah
Supper, and at our meeting an exciting program reviewing
books that every Jewish home should have . . . plus the
National Convention report.
Orlando on May 5, 6 and 7 and was attended by Mrs.
Herbert Lewitt, one of the Branch Vice-presidents, Mrs.
Louis Slott, Mrs. Norman Slaff, Mrs. Selwyn Savage and
Mrs. Mitchell Peltz.
Funds which enable us to fulfill our obligations to the
Center are obtained from our Gift Shop, sale of Barton’s
candy, World Book Encyclopedia and extra activities such as
the Georgia-Florida Dinner Dance and the Purim Ball.
The coming year will bring a new look to our program
as we present the musical “Carmen Shapiro” in November
and a very exciting Chanukah “Fair” on the 1st of Decem¬
ber.
We urge your continued support and may we, of Sister¬
hood, wish you and your dear ones a very wonderful year.

The revitalized Center Men’s Club under the direction of
Jack F. Shorstein this past year had the youth and drive to
make it one of the best years the Men’s Club has enjoyed.
Primary emphasis was placed on the monthly dinner meet¬
ings with topnotch programs throughout the year under the
capable chairmanship of Stanley Kantor.
The annual Membership Affair was the highlight of the
year with Michael Donziger making it one of the top social
affairs of the year. Another annual affair which has become
one of the long awaited days each year is the Picnic held
jointly this past year by the Men’s Club and Sisterhood
under the capable direction of Milton Felder and Robert
Shmunes of the Men’s Club.
In addition to providing a fine opportunity for fellow¬
ship, the Men’s Club provides scholarships for Camp
Ramah, a Blood Bank, adult education program, sponsors
Sunday Religious School picnic, supports the Sunday morn¬
ing Minyan breakfasts, and provides awards for the Scout¬
ing program along with scholarships to Camp Philmont.
With the second year under the able administration of
these veteran officers, the Club looks forward to an even
more dynamic program and a large membership drive to
increase their current roll of 180 strong. The membership
drive is now under way and all interested men are en¬
couraged to contact one of the officers or the Center
Office.
Scenes from Annual Sunday School Picnic sponsored jointly by Men’s Club and Sisterhood.

Building Fund Committee
Chairman: Louis Safer Treasurer: Louis Portnoy
Through the efforts of the Land Acquisition Committee under the co-chairmanship of Dr. Neal Roth and Sam Spevak,
the Center has recently acquired for its future needs a 36 acre site on State Road #13, 2/2 miles south of Beauclerc Coun¬
try Club. In planning the future of our dynamic Synagogue-Center, the Board feels that this large site will enable us to fully
nurture the type of program that our members desire.
This past year witnessed the refurbishing of our entire Youth Activities Building and the complete painting of the ex¬
terior of the entire Synagogue and Activities Buildings. In striving to maintain our building in top functional condition, the
air conditioning system was increased from its original capacity of 80 tons of cooling to a new capacity of over 200 tons.
We hope that our membership will make maximum use of our facilities and enjoy the comforts of our beautiful structure
with its new adequate cooling system.
Our members can feel proud of past accomplishments and we urge our members to continue their loyal support of the
Building Fund so that our physical facilities will always be a credit to our congregation.

Know Your Synagogue Center
¿6
The Jacksonville Jewish Center is
The Conservative Synagogue in Jacksonville
Scope of Activities
For over 60 years the Center has provided a modern
program in Jewish experience, ever increasing its services
and facilities. Our Year Book reviews the programs and
activities we make available for religious inspiration, edu¬
cation, social and athletic recreation, and Jewish living.
These are only too well known to our membership. For
the benefit of new members and newcomers to the city,
we set forth our program in brief. We also call attention
that the name “Center55 is to be distinguished from or¬
ganizations by similar names in other communities that
are primarily recreational Centers. We are a “Synagogue-
Center”.
Religious Services
Daily: Mornings, 7:30 A.M. (8:30 on Sundays)
Evenings, at sunset (including the eves of Sab¬
baths and Festivals)
Sabbath and Festivals: Mornings, 8:45 A.M.
Evenings, 8:00 P.M.
High Holidays: Members and their families, service¬
men and out-of-town guests are accommodated in our
air-conditioned Auditorium which seats 1400.
Personal Observances
Our religious leadership supplements public worship
with services on personal occasions at the Synagogue and
home, such as: Circumcision, naming of girls, Pidyon Ha-
ben, Bar Mitzvah, Bas Mitzvah, Confirmations, Mar¬
riages, Conversion to Judaism, Bereavement.
Education
Daily Kindergarten: Monday through Friday, 9 to 12
noon.
Hebrew School: Classes for boys and girls meeting on
Mon. through Thursday.
Foundation School: An all day intensive 1st and 2nd
grade of secular and Hebrew instruction.
Sunday Religious School: Sun. mornings, 10 to 12
noon; Pre-Kindergarten through Confirmation Class.
Youth Congregations: Sabbath and Festival mornings,
services at 10 A.M. held in separate Chapels accord¬
ing to grade.
Minyan Club: Sunday mornings at 8:45 for boys of
Bar Mitzvah age, followed by Breakfast.
Adult Courses: Lecture series are presented as available.
Institutes on specific subjects are arranged in con-
JACKSONVILLE JEWISH CENTER SYNAGOGUE
venient units of study. Special classes and discussion
groups are arranged as called for.
Youth Activities
Youth Groups: United Synagogue Youth, organized in
two chapters, Center Youth League and Charles Palot
Club, Major’s Pre-Teens for 7th and 8th graders and
Tween-Agers, for 5th and 6th graders.
Scouting: Boy Scouts and Cub Scouts, Girl Scouts and
Brownies.
Day Camp: A nine week summer program for boys
and girls age 4 through 13 and a C.I.T. class for
boys and girls age 14 and 15.
Extension Services
Affiliated Organizations: Sisterhood, Men’s Club, “32
Club”, Amity Club, Mr. and Mrs. Club, Single Adults
Group, “Knights and Dames”. Each of these organiza¬
tions is described in appropriate sections of the Center
Year Book.
Athletics: Programs for youth and adults, men and
women, conducted at our Gym and outdoors and also
at various community facilities.
Social Events: Scheduled by the various organizations
on special occasions or in connection with regular
worship and meetings.
Community Service: The Center’s facilities are avail¬
able not only for the celebration of personal events,
such as weddings, and Bar or Bas Mitzvahs, but are
also used by youth and adult organizations, civic
groups and inter-faith groups.
Cemeteries: Memorial Parks located at Evergreen Ave¬
nue and Winona Drive and at 43 rd Street east of
Main Street are owned and maintained by the con¬
gregation.
Center Bulletin: The “Center Bulletin” appears weekly
throughout the year and is mailed to our membership
and to interested persons whose names and addresses
are submitted to the office.
Center Year Book: For a fuller description of the
Center program and activities, the Center Year Book
published annually on the eve of Rosh Hashanah
offers a bird’s eye view of events for the preceding 12
month period, vital statistics of the membership, a
membership directory, directory of community organi¬
zations, Hebrew Religious Calendar for the coming
year, and many other items of interest.

Camp Ramah
Israeli Dance Scene at Camp Ramah in Connecticut.
Richard Browdy Linda Halpern
Our 1963 Camp Ramah participants: front row, 1. to r.:
Eliot Safer, Leonard Selber, Mayer Becker; 2nd row: Gail
Price, Rose Raskin, Debbie Lederman and Sheila Fages;
back row: Michael Schneider, Simon Selber and Simeon
Goldblum.
For the first time our two Camp Ramah graduates pic¬
tured at left, Richard Browdy and Linda Halpern, were
selected to be among the 75 members to spend a two month
period in Israel with Camp Ramah. We hope that this will
be an incentive for our future graduates of Camp Ramah
as the culmination of their Hebrew School—Camp Ramah
program.
Adult Education
The great response to our adult education program this
past year has enabled us to foster a weekly Wednesday
evening course of study with six diversified study groups. In
addition to these weekly classes, the Adult Education par¬
ticipants and the congregation also took part in the South¬
east Region Cavalcade on adult education.
Some of the outstanding speakers representing the United
Synagogue of America who participated in this Regional
Cavalcade were Rabbi Mordecai Waxman who spoke on
“The Heritage and Its Heirs”; Rabbi Abraham Karp on
“Adult Education”; Dr. Walter Ackerman on “Latest
Trends in Jewish Education”; Rabbi Raphael Arzt on
“Camp Ramah”; and Dr. Ira Eisenstein on “Reconstruc¬
tionism”.
Jewish Theological Seminary
The Jewish Theological Seminary of America in New
York is an academy of learning which keeps alive the values
and traditions of Judaism symbolized by the Eternal Light.
It is a fountainhead—for through its teachers, scholars, and
leaders come the dynamic interpretation of Judaism that
inspires, furthers creative Jewish life and encourages inter¬
group understanding.
This, the 50th anniversary of the National Movement of
Conservative Judaism, marks the successful era of 50 years
of dedicated service to the Conservative Synagogues through¬
out the United States. This milestone will be celebrated at
the National Jubilee Convention held Nov. 17 through the
21st, 1963 at the Concord Hotel in New York. The entire
Conservative Movement and its lay leadership is urged to
participate in this national undertaking to mark 50 years of
progress.
The Camp Ramah program pictured on this page is
just one of the many projects that the Seminary has helped to
foster and nourish since its inception. The Seminary also
maintains the Jewish Museum, Eternal Light program,
Rabbinical School, Library, United Synagogue Youth and
many other undertakings, national in its scope through the
United Synagogue of America.

Religious Education
A summary of the school’s progress should contain two essentials: a high goal
showing that the school is never static, and progressive innovations to show how this
goal is being achieved.
The goal of the Jacksonville Jewish Center school is to have the highest stand¬
ard in the country. The methods pursued during the past year include the following:
Continuous education from 3 to 18 years of age.
Advanced departments.
A Foundation School of First and Second grades
Sunday, daily and Saturday classes of Hebrew.
Maximum use of available time.
Separated congregational levels on Saturday and Sunday.
Special tutoring during vacation time.
Enrichment programs as assemblies and library research.
Pupils who can take over all pulpit skills.
Introduction of experimental programs as team teaching and latest texts.
TED GROSS, Principal

The Foundation School curriculum was vastly enriched
this year in both secular and religious departments. Regular bi¬
weekly trips were made to the Public Library where the
children were allowed to select books for home reading and
monthly field trips were made to various places of interest
in and around the city.
As part of their regular Hebrew studies, the children en¬
joyed a Chanukah party planned by a mothers’ committee of
the PTA. To celebrate Purim a delightful program was pre¬
sented by the students for their parents and friends after
which a delicious luncheon was enjoyed by all. At Pesah
time a model Seder was held at River Garden Home to the
delight of the residents who attended.

Youth Activities
Center Youth Organizations
Center Youth League—(CYL)—A co-ed USY Chapter for 11th and 12th graders,
meeting every 2nd and 4th Sunday with varied programs from September
to May.
Charles Palot Club—(CPC^USY Chapter for 9th and 10th grade boys and
girls, meeting throughout the school year on the 2nd and 4th Sunday at
the Center.
Bob Fischer
Director
Dr. Jack Rosner
Chairman,
Youth Activities
United Synagogue Youth with which the above two clubs are af¬
filiated is served by the Southeast Region Office, Rabbi Allen Rutchik,
Director, 1075-95th St., Miami Beach 54, Fla.; National Headquarters,
Dr. Morton Siegel, Director, 1123 Broadway, #803, New York 10, N. Y.
Major’s Pre-Teens—A co-ed youth club for 7th and 8th graders with
its programs geared to this Pre-teen level, with varied social,
religious and athletic functions held monthly.
Stanley Kantor
USY Chairman
Tween-Agers—Boys and girls of the 5 th and 6th grades
participate in this our youngest club monthly.
Boy Scouts, Troop 14—For boys 11 to 14, meeting weekly
on Tuesday evenings.
Cub Scouts, Pack 14—Boys 8 to 11 participate in this
organization, meeting each Friday afternoon at Den
Mothers’ homes with monthly Pack meetings on the
last Thursday of the month.
Girl Scouts, Troops 6, 20 and 193—For girls who have out¬
grown the Brownies and wish to continue Scouting.
Brownie Troops 210, 269—A Scouting organization for
younger girls with weekly gatherings.
Byron Morris
Day Camp
Co-Chairman

summer program which is open to all children from 4 through 13. For
the 14 & 15 year olds we offer a Counselor-in-Training Program—with
on the job experience. This past summer 370 campers took advantage
of our summer activities.

United Synagogue Youth

Miss Evelyn Brauner,
S. Robert Shorstein
Center Youth League Officers
Another banner year in the annals of our oldest USY
group sponsored by the Center has been recorded. The bi¬
weekly meetings starting at 7:30 p.m. consist of a half hour
business meeting, a varied and diversified program for 45
minutes, followed by social or Israeli dancing until 9:30
p.m. One can see these youngsters on the 2nd and 4th
Sundays of each month in the Center’s Youth Activities
Building participating in various workshops, getting a news¬
paper out on time or participating in one of their many
Judo Exhibition following one of the USY Meetings by
the Bill Beach Judo Academy.
discussion groups which áre held at the various meetings.
Several items of interest were accomplished this year,
mainly:
• A new set of by laws was ratified by the membership
and put into use.
• One of our members was elected to President of the
Southeast Region at the Regional Convention in
Montgomery, Ala. during May of 1963, with 31 of our
members attending.
• 19 delegates represented the group at the annual
L.T.I.
The annual Best Boy and Best Girl Awards were presented
to Mitchell Perin and Linda Halpern at our annual Banquet
held in May. Our Building Spiritual Bridges program was
supported by the sale of snowballs, waiting on tables, car
wash, sale of name labels, etc. which made it possible for us
to contribute $109.00 to the National USY fund raising
project.
The delegates to L.T.I. this year from the C.Y.L. were
as follows: Karen Caslar, Dotty Garten, Herbert Gartner,
Phyllis Glossman, Richard Kidder, Ann Klein, Sheila
Kramer, Susan Krivan, Madelyn Levin, Anita Mésnick,
Cheryl Morse, Jerry Portnoy, Joe Saportas, Jerry Saferj
Lauren Silverberg, Harold Sutton, Francine Trager, Bob
Wolf son and Bruce Yergin, with Mitchell Perin a Counselor-
in-Training.
Our appreciation to Mrs. Ann Lewis for her guidance
throughout the year. Our advisors for 1963-64 will be Miss
Evelyn Brauner and S. Robert Shorstein.

Treasurer Mindy Marks
Officers of Charles Palot Club
After a trial period of splitting our membership into a
boys5 and girls’ group, this 9th and 10th grade club voted
to reunite and continue its organization as a co-ed USY
group.
Their programs during the past year included several
outstanding topics, i.e., handwriting expert, Religious Editor
of the Times^Union, Judo exhibition, long distance tele¬
phone call, a discussion with the Rabbi, etc. The group had
a large delegation at the Regional Convention in Mont¬
gomery and are sending 12 delegates to the summer L.T.I.
held annually at Gamp Blue Star in August.
In its fund raising program the Club had several projects
and also contributed $109.00 to make Jacksonville’s total
contribution $209 to the National B.S.B. program. Many
thanks are due to our advisors, Enid Goldberg, Evelyn
Brauner and Pepi Spielberger for their efforts on behalf of
our organization.
The following are the delegates from G.P.C. to L.T.I. at
Camp Blue Star in Hendersonville, N. Car.: Peggy Cohen,
Leslie Edwards, Dolly Fleet, Barbara Gefen, Gilda Goldman,
Hilda Goldman, Beverly Hart, Wendy Klein, Jack Mizrahi,
Donald Safer, Billy Wilensky and Leon Yergin.
Our leaders for 1963-64 will be Stanley Kan tor, Enid
Goldberg and Ada Hinda Block.
Bob Fischer addressing the annual USY
Banquet held jointly in May by CPC
and CYL.

TWEEN-AGERS
Our youngest group affiliated with our Youth Activities
Program is composed of youngsters in the fifth and sixth
grades. Each month the group enjoys a social activity such as
bowling, ice skating, a picnic or a Hanukah Dance Party.
The Club’s success is due mainly to their advisor, Mrs.
Bernard Glossman, and to the mothers’ committee consist¬
ing of Mesdames Seymore Hoffenberg, Manuel Katz, Byron
Morris and Ben Zimmerman. Special thanks go to our CPC
sponsors Lauren Silverberg, Gail Price and Howard Rosen¬
blatt.
The co-ordinator for 1963-64 will be Mrs. Bernard
Glossman with a mothers’ committee of Mesdames Seymore
Hoffenberg, Byron Morris and Ben Zimmerman.
MAJOR’S PRE-TEENS
Following a major revision this past year, the Major’s
Pre-Teens will now consist of 7th and 8th grade boys and
girls to make this organization a two year grade span as all
our other clubs at the Center. This practice has been found
the most suitable for this age level, with their programs
geared to the pre-teen level in monthly social, religious and
athletic affairs.
Many thanks are due Mrs. M. J. Reiser, advisor to the
group, for her tireless help and to the mothers’ committee,
Mrs. Byron Morris, Mrs. Seymour Morse, Mrs. Irwin Gend-
zier. Special thanks are also due the sponsors, Melvin
Gottlieb and Susan Krivan.
Mothers’ committee for 1963-64 will be under the chair¬
manship of Mrs. M. J. Reiser with Mesdames Irwin
Gendzier, Manuel Katz, David Paikin and Isidore Weiss.
A scene at the annual joint Banquet held in May by Tween-Agers and Major’s Pre-Teens—Rabbi Goldblum speaking.

Boy Scout Troop 14
OFFICERS
Scoutmaster Dr. Harry Yergin
Assistant Scoutmasters.. Allie Goldstein, Stephen Hackel, Milton Hecht and
William Rose
Chairman of Scout Committee Dr. Arnold Silverberg
Committee Members Herbert Weinstein and Norman Moss
Treasurer Robert Weiss
Secretary Bob Fischer
Institutional Representative... H. Ted Freedman
Dr. Harry Yergin,
Scoutmaster
Troop 14 composed of 46 Scouts enjoyed another out¬
standing year of scouting and camping. Camping trips were
made to Camp Echochotee, Crandall Point and Lake
Margie Ranch. At the fall Mohawk District Camp-o-Ree
the Troop 14 patrols were the winners of one blue, two red
and two yellow ribbons with our Troop making an over-all
good showing in the weekend games.
20 Scouts of our Troop attended the annual summer
camp at Camp Echochotee for a period of two weeks. Three
senior Scouts Allen Harris, Michael Weiss and Martin Ber¬
man were tapped by and later inducted into the Order of
the Arrow, an honorary Scout organization consisting of
outstanding Scouts. Our congratulations to these three boys.
As in the past we are again proud of our outstanding Ner
Tamid class as seven Scouts received this highest religious
achievement: Sheldon Moss, Charles Goldblum, Eliot Safer,
Allen Harris, Michael Zadoff, Richard Weiss, Michael
Weiss. We are also proud of the following Scouts who
earned the right to wear the rank of Eagle Scout—Avery
Weiss, Sheldon Moss and Richard Weiss.
At our annual Blue and Gold Banquet we had an at¬
tendance of over 250 parents and Scouts. Attending as
guests were Scoutmasters of Troops 12 of the Temple and
275 of Etz Chaim, together with their committee chairmen.
(Editor’s Note) We would be amiss if this edition of the
Center Year Book did not make a special note of the devoted
service of our Scoutmaster, Dr. Harry Yergin, for the past
several years. His knowledge and leadership of our Troop
has made it one of the outstanding Troops in the Mohawk
District and North Florida Council. His work has been
recognized by Scout Headquarters on numerous occasions
and by the Center Troop Committee. Our heartfelt thanks
for his continued efforts on behalf of our youth.
Our 1963 class of Ner Tamid recipients with Rabbi Goldblum; Dr. Harry Yergin, Scout¬
master; Norman Moss, Committee Member and Louis Safer, President.

Recipients of Best Cub awards for 1962-63
Den Mothers: Left to right—Den 2, Mrs. Sidney Engelhardt;

Another successful year was ended for the Cub
Scouts with a Swim Party and Cook Out during June.
The 46 member Pack enjoyed a banner year with out¬
standing monthly Pack Meetings and an active Com¬
mittee under the capable chairmanship of Milton
Greenspan.
Several Den Mothers and Cuhmaster Sidney Engel¬
hardt have completed a training course given by the
Scout Office to enrich our leaders with the leadership
qualities needed for Cubbing.
The Pack meets on the last Thursday of each
month at the Center throughout the whole year and
each Friday during the school year at the respective Den
Mothers5 homes. Our sincere thanks to the Pack Com¬
mittee and the Den Mothers for a yeoman’s job well
done.

The Center’s three Girl Scout Troops and two Brownie
Troops completed another successful year with their an¬
nual Banquet held in June. Several awards and recogni¬
tions were presented at the Banquet with Mrs. Bond of
the Girl Scout office praising the Center’s sponsorship of
our five troops.
Sincere appreciations go to our Troop leaders and
their co-leaders for the devoted task which they perform
during the year. The Troops meet weekly at the leaders’
homes with groups located in each section of the city.
Girl Scout Troop 6 with leader Mrs. Irvine Kowitz.

from Girl Scout Headquarters, Mrs. Leon Schinasi, Mrs.
Weinstein and Mr. Beryl Weinstein.

Mr. and Mrs. Club
OFFICERS
President Mr. and Mrs. Henry (Ted) Freedman
First Vice-President....Mr, and Mrs. Harold Pausman*
Second Vice-President Mr. and Mrs. Ted Friedman

Aaron Shapiro
Past President Mr. and Mrs. Sidney Berkowitz
* Not Pictured
The Mr. and Mrs. Club, a social organization now in
its tenth year, meets at the Center for its social and business
activities. The Club which limits its membership to 75
couples fosters congeniality and good fellowship among its
loyal membership, who form monthly committees to conduct
the dinner and dance affairs each month.
The committees vie with each other in preparing food,
entertainment and decorations in order to earn an “Itzik”
Officers and Board of the Mr. and Mrs. Club
award in the various categories. A family picnic affair is held
in the summer months, an Installation Banquet mid-year
and a New Year’s Eve Party highlight the social whirl
conducted by the Club.
Each member is expected to serve at least once a year on
a monthly committee. Membership in the Club is by ap¬
plication and those interested may contact any of the
Officers or Board members.

Now in its third year of operation, this young social
club for married couples with either spouse being under the
age of 32, completed another successful year. The monthly
affairs under the advisorship of the Vice-President and a
different member of the Board are in charge along with a
committee composed of the membership at large. Each
member and his wife is asked to work on one affair per
year. The highlight of our year’s activities is the extravagant
New Year’s Eve Party held at the Robert Meyer Hotel with
two bands and an outstanding entertainment bill.
Our organization is open to all married couples and any¬
one interested is asked to contact our membership chairmen,
Mr. and Mrs. Sy Bellack, 725-8513, or Mr. and Mrs.
Lawrence Dubow, FL 9-9054.

Single Adults Club
OFFICERS
Chairman Phyllis Foor
Assistant Chairman Sandra Freedman
Treasurer Susan Margolin
Secretary Vicky Gaon
Advisor Bob Fischer
Since its inception in January of 1963 this young single group has grown to a
membership of 70 members with monthly affairs under the chairmanship of the
current officers. The monthly functions are held at various locations as per the
theme of the affair. Some of the events this past year included a Party at the Robert Meyer Hotel Pool, a Splash at the
Julington Creek Marina, a Get-Acquainted Party at the Center and a Cocktail Party at the home of Leon Rosenberg.
The group is open to all single people between 18 and 30 years of age. Anyone interested is asked to contact any of
the above officers.
Newly elected officers for the 1963-64 season are Frank Freedman, Chairman; Barbara Silverman, Assistant Chairman
and Martin Esroff, Treasurer.
Phyllis Foor, Chairman

Jacksonville Jewish Community Council
ROBERT I. MARCUS
President ... Al L. Schneider
Exec. Vice-Pres Alvin Leitman

Hon. Presidents Alexander Brest, P. N. Cole¬

W. Wolfson, Edgar Felson
AL L. SCHNEIDER
Serving the entire Jewish community of Jacksonville, the
Jewish Community Council is the central Welfare Fund and
Community Relations body of the local Jewish community.
An association of individuals, synagogues, organizations and
social service agencies, the program encompasses key ac¬
tivities which give unity, cohesion and coordination to local
Jewish communal life.
A communityrwide annual Welfare Fund Campaign pro¬
vides support to significant local, national and overseas or¬
ganizations. The National United Jewish Appeal, has ex¬
pressed American Jewry’s responsibility for the welfare and
rehabilitation needs of Jewish communities the world over
for over 20 years. River Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged
and the Jewish Welfare Society are the local beneficiaries.
The Council publishes a monthly newspaper known as
the Commentator. It is the official organ of the Council and
its member organizations.
The Council conducts a leisure time activities program
which includes Youth Council programs such as college
preparation workshops, a youth news page in the Com¬
mentator, Sunday sports and a youth division in the annual
Jewish Welfare Fund Campaign. The Council also sponsors
a community summer Day Camp for children between the
ages of 4 and 15.

River Garden Hebrew
President Arthur J. Gutman
Executive Vice-President & Chairman Admissions...Samuel J. Thier
Vice-President & Co-Chairman Admissions... Frank Diamond
Vice-President & Co-Chairman Admissions Sheldon Gendzier

Chairman Board of Trustees & Honorary President... Joe Becker
This past year was truly a year of fulfillment as the Morris and
Sarah Wolf son Pavillion was built and completed.
“Opening the door for 50 more” brings the capacity of the
Home For The Aged
Arthur Gutman, President
Home to 120 residents. Increasing infirmities and disabilities among
the aged made it imperative to design the new building as an in¬
firmary with all the complexities of a hospital. It contains a clinic
wing with examining, dental, podiatry, physiotherapy, laboratory,
and x-ray rooms for specific services. The nursing divisions on the
second and third floors are equipped with an audio visual call
system connected from each bedroom to the nurse’s station. The
furnishings and decor of the bedrooms are homelike and attractive.
To further the concept of the Home as a community activity
center for the aged, a large auditorium, solarium and chapel will
encourage public participation in events at River Garden.
Climaxing the story of the new building is the intangible value
of the beautiful scenic view of the St. Johns river from the 4th
story solarium and sun deck. The setting, the facilities and services
create for the aged a setting where they can find security and com¬
fort in the later years.

Ladies Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society
OFFICERS
President L Mrs. William Goldman
Vice-President Mrs. Roy J. Sloat
Vice-President Mrs. I. P. Edwards
Vice-President Mrs. Myron Rosenberg
Recording Secretary Mrs. William Silverman
Financial Secretary Mrs. Albert Kofka
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Sam Lazarus
Treasurer, Dues Mrs. Samuel J. Thier
Treasurer, Projects Mrs. Louis Witten
Ladies Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society was founded 40
years ago as an aid society to help the needy of the Jewish
faith. It was always the dream and hope of our founders to
have a Home for the Aged in Jacksonville. This dream be¬
came a reality when River Garden Hebrew Home for the
Aged was founded. Since that time, our role became that of
the auxiliary to the Home.
We provide extra-budgetary needs such as furnishings,
redecorating, air conditioning, kitchen and infirmary equip¬
ment. Funds for these needs were raised through Tombola,
Donor and Social Printing projects as well as annual dues
and life memberships in Ladies Aid. Linens were provided by
our annual Linen Shower.
We sponsor a Grey Ladies Program, regularly scheduled
entertainment and parties. River Garden residents participate
in projects served by the auxiliary such as the residents’
OFFICERS
magazine, Ceramic classes and the Hobby Shop.
The Golden Sunshine Club of River Garden which
emerged from our weekly Senior Citizens meetings has
grown into a self-sufficient organization giving fellowship
and companionship as well as participating in civic and
philanthropic activities.
This year Ladies Aid pledges to continue its efforts and
cooperation in bringing not only the material but the
spiritual values that our elder citizens hold so dear.

Jewish Family and Children’s Service
New Home of JFCS
President Jack Coleman
Vice-Presidents........ Lewis Ansbacher, Nathan Kazin,
Dr. Morris Price
Treasurer David Moss
Executive Director Lawrence Rackow
The Jewish Family and Children’s Service is now located
in beautiful new quarters at 1415 LaSalle St. where it will
be better able to serve the Jewish community with warmth,
dignity and convenience. This new and spacious building
is off Hendricks Ave., directly west of the Southside Library,
phone 359-2941.
JFCS has a program which can be listed under seven
major categories:
1.Family and individual casework: marital, emotional,
economic, health and aging problems.
Mr. Jack Coleman
2. Child welfare, to maintain children in their own
homes with their own family, or to license, maintain and
supervise them in foster homes or in adoptive homes.
3. Counselling and maintaining unmarried mothers and
placing their children for adoption.
4. Processing applications and providing case work for
the residents of River Garden Home for the Aged.
5. Resettlement of New Americans with funds provided
by the Jewish Community Council.
6. Financial assistance to local residents and transients.
7. A counselling service to Jewish prisoners at Raiford.
Anticipated new service will be the training and coordi¬
nation of volunteers in a friendly visiting program to aged
in their own homes.

Zionist Organization of America
Jacksonville District
OFFICERS
President Henry Bettman
1st Vice-President Myer Cohen
2nd Vice-President Harry Libby
3rd Vice-President Albert Meyerhoff
Treasurer Louis Moss
Secretary Charles Klugose
Membership Chairman David Moss
Membership Co-Chairman Sidney Weiss
Education and Program Chairman Jack Becker
Chairman of the Board Cantor A. Marton
Israel Bonds Max M. Zemes
Public Relations Isadore Moscovitz
Jewish National Fund Max Rubin
Ex Officio H I. M. Lieberman
The essential function of the Zionist Organization of
America is to keep alive in this country an interest in the
State of Israel. This interest is many-sided, as the efforts of
Zionism always were. The political objective was the creation
of a Jewish State. To this end it was necessary to revive the
Hebrew language, acquire land, train colonists for settle¬
ment and raise funds to finance the project. All this the
Zionist movement did and our district organization received
Z.O.A. BOARD
a current report from its delegates who attended the Ameri¬
can Zionist Convention which was held in Israel this sum¬
mer.
Today, the Z.O.A. continues to foster support for the
State of Israel. Moreover, the Z.O.A. seeks to strengthen
the bonds between American Jewry and the Jewish State on
the basis of the cultural tradition that is our common
heritage.
Religious differences present no obstacle as Zionism en¬
compasses all shades of religious belief in every global area.
The Z.O.A. invites all Jewish men to join its ranks.

Senior Hadassah
OFFICERS
President
Vice-President
Vice-President
Vice-President
Vice-President
Treasurer
Mrs. Henry Cristol
{Programming) Mrs. Maurice Goldstein

Mrs. Monroe Klausner
Financial Secretary Mrs. Jack Eisenberg
Recording Secretary Mrs. Edward Seigel
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Norman Moss
Hadassah was founded in 1912 by Henrietta Szold, and from
a mere handful of dedicated American women, has grown to a
membership of over 318,000.
Hadassah projects are actively functioning on two far-flung
horizons. On the American scene, an Educational Program, a
Youth Activities program, the Young Judeans and the Junior
Hadassah, Hadassah co-sponsors with Z.O.A. Camp Judaea, a sum¬
mer camp in Hendersonville, North Carolina. In Israel, Hadassah
encompasses Hadassah Medical Organization, Medical Center,
Youth Aliyah, Vocational Education, and Jewish National Fund.
By helping Israel help itself, so that it can take its place with
dignity among the freedom-loving nations of the world, Hadassah
reaffirms the Jewish faith in the heritage of freedom.
Two groups comprise the Jacksonville Chapter: the Senior
Hadassah and the Hannah Senesch Evening Group. Almost 800
women boast membership in the local chapter, and annually seek
Officers of Hadassah
to raise quotas totaling nearly $20,000 in order to meet commit¬
ments largely in Israel, as well as in America.
Senior Hadassah meets every second Tuesday of each month
from October through May. This coming year’s calendar offers a
variety of activities with a well-rounded educational and social pro¬
gram to interest every member. The Opening Luncheon meeting in
October will highlight an original adaptation of a current Broadway
musical, and is the forerunner of many more stimulating meetings
and exciting affairs during the forthcoming year.
Hannah Senesch-Evening Group of Hadassah
EXECUTIVE BOARD
President Mrs. David Moed
Vice-President {Program) Mrs. Max Brownstein
Vice-President {Fund-Raising).. Mrs. Meyer Richman
Vice-President (Membership) Mrs. Michael Arab
Vice-President {Education) Mrs. Alfred Rosenszweig
Treasurer Mrs. Joe Sheff
Financial Secretary Mrs. Sarah Bossen
Recording Secretary Miss Bertha Mehlman
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. William Gordon
This group is geared for participation by the Business or Pro¬
fessional woman, as well as the non-working woman who also wishes
to avail herself of the opportunity to join in evening meetings and
activities. Although its primary purpose is to further Hadassah’s
vast humanitarian program, this congenial group intersperses a bit
of sociability along with its fund-raising efforts.
Meetings are held in the Jewish Center on the second Monday
evening of each month, to which all interested persons are invited.
Hannah Senesch Board

Young Judaea
Jerry Linsey
Pres. Southern
Region
Paula Saraga
Tova
Jonathan Marcus

Hashomerim
Young Judaea, sponsored by Hadassah and Z.O.A., has completed a
prosperous and successful year. Young Judaea activities included Israeli
dancing at the Civic Auditorium, three regional conventions, and various
religious and social programs.
Thirteen Jacksonville Judaeans attended the Southern Region Sum¬
mer Convention at Camp Judaea in Hendersonville, N. C. in June. The
following were elected to serve as officers: Jerry Linsey, Southern Region
President; Howard Moscovitz, Southern Region Vice-President; Michael
Pousman, National Representative; Ira Brukner, Regional Programming
Committee; Evelyn Goldstein, “Miss Southern Region.”
Camp Judaea in Hendersonville is a major project of Young Judaea.
Hadassah and Z. O. A. sponsor this 4 or 8 week camp period with a full
camping and Zionist program. Camp Judaea serves to develop in youth
the ideals of American freedom, an appreciation of the Jewish people,
a bond with the land of Israel and an understanding of the religious
and ethical concepts of Judaism.
Club leaders for the coming year are Carol Feinberg, Bas Yisrael;
Mrs. I. Brukner and Jerry Linsey, T.A.C., Sybil Horovitz, Tova.
Gilda Goldman

Esquire Club

For twenty-seven years the Esquire Club has been a leading social club for
young men in Jacksonville. Interests of the club include social, athletic, cultural and
religious activities. Outstanding social events were the annual Holiday Dance and the
Western Hayride. Esquire is a member of the Jacksonville Youth Council, an af¬
filiate of the Jacksonville Jewish Community Council.
Brent Turbow
Newly-elected officers are planning a new and revitalized fall program which
promises to be the most outstanding in recent years. With the assistance of our
popular sweetheart, Miss Mary Friedman, and our advisors, Esquire expects 1963-64
to be its greatest year.

jB’nai B’rith, A. B. Weil Lodge ISo. 800
President Paul J. Bossen

Committee Chairmen: Irwin “Bud” Goldberg, Bert Whitfield, Lionel Gaines, I.
“Scotty” Witten, David Snider, Morton Hirschberg, Joseph B. Hyde, Jack

B’NAI B’RITH’s mission is to unite persons of the Jewish faith in the work of
promoting their highest interests and those of humanity. This we do through
service, study and benevolence.
Our programs include A.D.L., Hillel, BBYO, Vocational Guidance, Adult
Jewish Education, Citizenship, Veterans and Community Affairs projects, Scholar¬
ship Fund and support of national B’NAI B’RITH philanthropies.
Paul J. Bossen, President
B’nai B’rith Women
President Mrs. Norman Danglo
Vice-Presidents: Program Mrs. Sumner Waitz
Ways & Means Mrs. Gus Goldstein
Membership Mrs. Marty Caplan
Treasurer Mrs. Mac Presser
Recording Secretary Mrs. Max Mack

Social Secretary .... Mrs. Sam Lazarus
Historian Mrs. William Newman
Chaplain Mrs. Sam Rosenberg
Auditor Mrs. Irving Resnick
Counselor Mrs. Martin Brown
B’nai B’rith Women are dedicated to serving you, your
family, your country and humanity. This is carried through
by supporting different national B’nai B’rith philanthropies
such as Belief aire Children’s Center; Leo N. Levi Memorial
Hospital; National Jewish Hospital and B’nai B’rith Center
for Jewish patients at Mayo Clinic. B’nai B’rith Women
also sponsors the Children’s Home in Israel and other Israeli
projects. Locally, our activities include: the Anti-Defamation
League (“Dolls for Democracy” program, distribution of
Board of B’nai B’rith Women
memo calendar books to the public schools and keeping
members aware of the problems in the Jewish Community);
teaching home economics at Pinecastle School; helping at
River Garden Home; sponsoring the B.B. Girls and their
Vocational Guidance Service; Hillel at U. of Fla.; and
helping in all community drives. In the coming year we
hope to make the name B’NAI B’RITH synonymous with
our motto: Service, Fidelity and Devotion.

Six-fold Chairmen: Gail Mack, Marsha
Zaret, Karen Weiner, Vicki Rubin,
Terry Barnert, Phyllis Chamison.
B’nai B’rith Girls, the future B’nai B’rith Women, enjoyed a marvelous and ex¬
citing year.
Many of our functions of the past year include a Car Mazuzah Sale, a Heaven
and Hell Dance, discussions on the A.D.L., People to People Plan, and the Amigo
Plan. Also, our College Convention here, in which we entertained 100 BBYO’ers
from North Florida, was a fantastic success.
We are also very proud of our Past-President, Louise Hilsenrad, on being appointed
head of the “Amigo Plan” in Fla., at District Convention in Hendersonville, N.G.
We ended the year with our Annual End-of-the-year Party and then our Installa¬
tion Luncheon, at which the Best Girl Award was presented to Louise Gutman.
We can truthfully say that our Order is taking its share of the responsibility to
secure peace for ourselves and all the peoples of the world. At present we are
working on a Latin American student exchange program, Operation Amigo, Inc.
In the coming year, thirty students from Latin America will, for a period of two
weeks, attend Englewood High School and live with American families. This pro¬
gram is now being carried out by BBYO’s all over the nation.
Other programs that we are working with such as People-To-People, Domestic
Peace Corps, and more, all have the same goal—everlasting peace for all mankind.
We would like to thank our advisor, Myron Blattner, for without his guidance,
we could not have had all the success we did. We would also like to thank all the
parents who have worked with us this year.
B’nai B’rith Girls

The National Council of Jewish Women is an organi¬
zation dedicated to furthering human welfare in the Jewish
and general communities, locally, nationally and interna¬
tionally.
NCJW Officers
The Evening Group
Some of our community projects are: Audiometer, test¬
ing the hearing of all first grade children in Duval County;
Juvenile Shelter, a planned year-round recreational pro¬
gram and furnishing books for their library; Medical Loan
Shelf, supply hospital and sickroom equipment to indigents
through VNA; Mental Health Fair, co-sponsored with the
Mental Health Association of Duval County; Sewing Com¬
mittee, makes layettes and baby clothes for the needy of the
community; Tribute Fund, purchase of hearing aids for
indigent children.
Chairman | Mrs. Dorothy Marx

Corres. Secretary Mrs. Dorothy Shane
Treasurer Mrs. Mary Katz

and provides programs at River Garden each month; makes
flash cards for the children in speech and hearing program;
packs boxes for children in Israel and Duval Medical
Center.

The Progressive Credit Union sponsored by the Jewish
Progressive Society is organized to encourage thrift and pro¬
vide a safe, convenient medium whereby members can invest
and accumulate savings. The Credit Union carries life sav¬
ings insurance recently increased to $2,000 and loan protec¬
tion insurance without any direct cost to the members. Last
year the Board declared a 3}4% dividend on savings and
10% rebate of interest to borrowing members.
Ten Commandments of our Credit Union: The Member
is the most important person in our Credit Union. The
Member is not an outsider—he is an owner of our Credit
Union. The Member is not an interruption of our work—he
is the purpose of it. The Member does us a favor when he

*AII days of the Jewish year begin at sundown. Therefore, each holiday
really begins at sundown of the day preceding our symbol.
ROSH HASHANAH (the Jewish New Year), a solemn two-day
holiday when the Shofar (ram’s horn) sounds forth in the
synagogue to call the people to repentance and to reawaken
responsibility to the Lord.

TEVET
15 TEVET
HANUKAH (Dedication) — Anniversary of the day when the Jews, led by
Judah Maccabee and his brothers, freed the Temple from Syrian invaders.
At the Temple’s rededication, only enough undefiled oil was found to light
the Holy Lamp for one day. Miraculously, it burned eight days. Thus, on
the first day of Hanukah, Jews light one candle of the Menorah (eight-
branched candlestick, our symbol), adding another flame each day until
all eight are lit.

SHEVAT
HAMISHA ASAR B’SHEVAT (Fifteenth Day of Shevat), the
New Year of the Trees. Often referred to as the Jewish
Arbor Day, this holiday commemorates the ancient need for
trees in the Holy Land. It is celebrated everywhere with appropriate songs
and games. Fruits long associated with the trees that once grew in the
Holy Land (and have begun to grow there again) are eaten on this day.

Lag Ba'omer
14 IYAR
15 IYAR
16 IYAR

IYAR 18
IYAR
PESAH (Passover), the Feast of Unleavened Bread—an eight-day celebra¬
tion of the deliverance of the Jews from Egyptian slavery. During the tra¬
ditional Seder meal, on the first two nights of Passover, Jewish families
read the Haggadah (a ritual recitation of the story of the Exodus).
Throughout the Passover periods, foods are selected according to special
dietary laws. Instead of bread, Jews eat matzoh (unleavened bread) to
recall the hasty flight from Egypt, when there was no time to allow the
bread to rise.

DR. BARRY ELLIN
Chiropractic is the second largest healing profes¬
sion in America. It is officially classified as one of
the four major healing professions, along with
medicine, dentistry and osteopathy, by the Execu¬
tive Office of the President of the United States,
Bureau of the Budget, Washington, D. C.
The first requisite of health procedure is the
maintenance of the mechanical integrity of the
body . . . free from spinal defects and postural dis¬
tortion ... in keeping with chiropractic principles.
The practice of chiropractic is as broad as the
nervous system which controls all organs, glands
and tissues of the body. Therefore, chiropractic is
applicable to a wide variety of diseases which af¬
fect the human body and mind.
Some 35 million Americans who have experi¬
enced the health benefits of chiropractic care agree
with eminent medical authorities that chiropractic
is pre-eminent in the field of health restoration
and maintenance.
When you want to enjoy better health — see
your doctor of chiropractic FIRST.
